# mRNA vaccines for infectious diseases — advances, challenges and opportunities

[Norbert Pardi](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/?term="Pardi%20N"[Author])

PMCID: PMC13101425  NIHMSID: NIHMS2152818  PMID: [39367276](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/39367276/) 

The publisher's version of this article is available at [Nat Rev Drug Discov](https://doi.org/10.1038/s41573-024-01042-y) 

## Abstract

The concept of mRNA-based vaccines emerged more than three decades ago. Groundbreaking discoveries and technological advancements over the past 20 years have resolved the major roadblocks that initially delayed application of this new vaccine modality. The rapid development of nucleoside-modified COVID-19 mRNA vaccines demonstrated that this immunization platform is easy to develop, has an acceptable safety profile and can be produced at a large scale. The flexibility and ease of antigen design have enabled mRNA vaccines to enter development for a wide range of viruses as well as for various bacteria and parasites. However, gaps in our knowledge limit the development of next-generation mRNA vaccines with increased potency and safety. A deeper understanding of the mechanisms of action of mRNA vaccines, application of novel technologies enabling rational antigen design, and innovative vaccine delivery strategies and vaccination regimens will likely yield potent novel vaccines against a wide range of pathogens.

## Introduction

Vaccines have had a significant impact on the reduction of morbidity and mortality caused by various pathogens[1](#R1). The introduction of comprehensive vaccine programmes in many countries in the second half of the twentieth century heralded a turning point in the battle against deadly pathogens, allowing nearly complete eradication of several serious childhood diseases and mitigation of a number of other dangerous viral or bacterial diseases[2](#R2). However, some of the currently licensed vaccines are unable to induce robust, broadly protective, durable immune responses, are time-consuming and difficult to produce, or have a suboptimal safety profile, highlighting the need for improvements[2](#R2). Additionally, human vaccines are currently lacking for multiple relevant hard-to-target pathogens, such as human immunodeficiency virus 1 (HIV-1) or hepatitis C virus, and a large number of other viruses and human pathogenic bacteria, fungi, and parasites that, combined, are responsible for millions of deaths worldwide every year.

mRNA vaccines are newcomers in the field of vaccinology. Decades of work have now addressed fundamental hurdles of in vitro-transcribed (IVT) mRNA, including its highly inflammatory nature and difficulties with safe and efficient in vivo delivery and large-scale production. Key advances include the introduction of modified nucleosides into the mRNA, the utilization of ionizable lipid nanoparticles (LNPs) for mRNA delivery, and optimization of the IVT reaction that allows cell-free, rapid, and scalable production of mRNA vaccines. Of note, this vaccine platform has several critical advantages over other modalities. First, the flexibility of antigen design (the mRNA sequence can be rapidly modified) makes mRNA vaccines ideal to target rapidly evolving pathogens such as influenza virus and severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2 (SARS-CoV-2). Second, the ease of mRNA production allows for a rapid iterative screening process to identify optimized immunogens against pathogens. Third, multicomponent (combined) mRNA vaccines can be made to target several different antigens of a single pathogen[3](#R3)–[7](#R7). For example, various versions of the same antigen were included in preclinical influenza virus vaccines[8](#R8) and bivalent licensed coronavirus disease 2019 (COVID-19) vaccines[9](#R9), and others targeted more than one pathogen with a single vaccine formulation[10](#R10),[11](#R11).

Fourth, mRNA vaccines can be produced at a large scale in a short time frame, which is highly desirable for seasonal (for example, influenza) vaccines or in a pandemic scenario[12](#R12). Fifth, mRNA vaccines represent an ideal platform for the design of vaccines targeting complex proteins with multiple transmembrane domains. Some of these critical features of mRNA vaccines made them ideal for the efficient response to the COVID-19 pandemic. Indeed, nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccines were quickly developed by Moderna and Pfizer–BioNTech and billions of doses were manufactured in a short time frame[13](#R13). Most importantly, both formulations proved to be highly effective in preventing death and severe COVID-19 and saved millions of lives globally during the SARS-CoV-2 pandemic ([Box 1](#BX1)).

### Box 1 |. mRNA steps into the spotlight: the success of COVID-19 mRNA vaccines.

Severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2 (SARS-CoV-2) emerged in late 2019 at a time when the nucleoside-modified mRNA–lipid nanoparticle (LNP) vaccine platform had just been validated by demonstrating its efficacy in animal studies[119](#R119),[120](#R120), mRNA vaccine candidates for different pathogens were in phase I and II trials, and Good Manufacturing Practice-grade raw materials for mRNA vaccine production had become widely available. Therefore, Moderna and Pfizer–BioNTech were able to design and produce their vaccine candidates very quickly after obtaining the sequence of the spike protein of the Wuhan strain of SARS-CoV-2 (ref. [93](#R93)). The first published clinical data sets demonstrated that two immunizations with either the mRNA-1273 (Moderna) or BNT162b2 (Pfizer–BioNTech) mRNA-LNP COVID-19 vaccine induced strong spike-specific neutralizing antibody responses in the vaccinees[226](#R226),[227](#R227). Several months later, phase III trials demonstrated safety and very high efficacy (~90% at up to 6 months of follow-up)[20](#R20),[21](#R21). Mass vaccinations with mRNA-1273 and BNT162b2 started between late December 2020 and January 2021 after both formulations received emergency use authorization from the FDA and the EMA. These two COVID-19 vaccines proved to be generally safe and effective in ‘real life’ as well as in preventing symptomatic disease and severe and deadly cases of COVID-19. With the appearance of new viral variants, SARS-CoV-2 mRNA-LNP vaccines were quickly updated by matching the sequence of the spike-encoding mRNA to that in the circulating viruses.

The nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccine made a significant impact during the COVID-19 pandemic. Billions of vaccine doses have been given to a total of more than 1 billion people as of April 2024. The success of COVID-19 mRNA vaccines has created opportunities for the development of a large number of novel mRNA-based infectious disease vaccines, cancer vaccines and mRNA-based therapeutics. However, it is important to note that improvements in the platform technology are needed, including strategies to increase immunogenicity, decrease reactogenicity, improve stability and others.

The past 10–15 years have brought tremendous advancements in vaccine research yielding novel immunization platforms, a greater understanding of the biology of multiple pathogens, new insights into human immunology, new vaccines against diseases like dengue, respiratory syncytial virus (RSV) and malaria (in addition to COVID-19) and, importantly, development of novel technologies that enable rational antigen design for the induction of optimal immune responses.

This Review provides an overview of mRNA-based vaccine platforms, including the principles of mRNA design and delivery technologies, assesses current understanding of the mechanisms of action of mRNA vaccines, and highlights novel preclinical and clinical infectious disease vaccine formulations. The most important challenges, limitations and opportunities in mRNA vaccine research as well as the potential of combining mRNA platform technology with reverse vaccinology to enable novel, safe and effective human vaccines against multiple pathogens, are discussed.

## Overview of mRNA vaccine technology

The majority of preclinical and all directly injectable clinical mRNA vaccines have two major components: the antigen-encoding mRNA and a delivery platform that protects it from rapid degradation by RNases and facilitates the cellular uptake of the large, negatively charged RNA molecules. Both components of mRNA vaccines influence the quality and magnitude of the elicited immune responses.

### RNA formats and the principles of RNA design

Over the past 25 years, several RNA formats have been developed and evaluated in preclinical and clinical studies ([Fig. 1](#F1)). Non-replicating (linear) mRNA, with or without modified nucleosides, and self-amplifying mRNA (saRNA) represent the major platforms that have been most extensively studied and improved[14](#R14),[15](#R15). Additional emerging RNA platforms include trans-amplifying mRNA and circular RNA ([Box 2](#BX2)).

#### Fig. 1 |. RNA vaccine platforms.

![Fig. 1 |](https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/cee2/13101425/16cfb06f3d62/nihms-2152818-f0001.jpg)

[Open in a new tab](figure/F1/)

The figure shows RNA formats for vaccination that are currently being used or explored. Of note, circular RNA is defined by the presence of an internal ribosome entry site (IRES) and the antigen-coding region. Untranslated regions (UTRs) can improve circular RNA, and are therefore shown in the figure. (A)n, poly(A) tail; m7G, 7-methylguanosine cap; nsP, non-structural protein.

#### Box 2 |. circRNA as a vaccine platform.

A growing body of knowledge demonstrates that circular RNAs (circRNAs) have diverse functions in a wide range of organisms (invertebrate and vertebrate animals, plants, fungi): they function as protein and gene sponges, cell activity modulators, or templates for protein translation[264](#R264). Synthetic circRNAs have recently been proposed as a novel RNA vaccine platform to induce protective immunity[264](#R264). CircRNA is usually generated by the synthesis of one or more precursor linear RNAs, followed by RNA circularization via chemical or enzymatic ligation methods[264](#R264). CircRNA is a closed RNA without a 5′ cap and 3′ poly(A) tail and is thus not considered a type of mRNA[265](#R265),[266](#R266) ([Fig. 1](#F1)). Owing to the lack of a 5′ cap, introduction of internal ribosome entry site elements is needed for translation from circRNA. The covalently closed structure of circRNA renders it resistant to degradation by exonucleases and potentially allows for an increased half-life compared to linear mRNA. This unique feature of circRNA vaccines can be advantageous over mRNA vaccines as sustained antigen production from the former may induce more robust and durable immune responses. circRNA can be extensively optimized for better translation by engineering the circRNA vector, 5′ and 3′ untranslated regions, or internal ribosome entry site elements or by utilizing synthetic adapters that support translation[267](#R267). Although circRNA can be administered in vivo without delivery molecules, nanocarriers are likely necessary for more efficient cargo delivery and generation of robust immune responses. A seminal study used a lipid nanoparticle-encapsulated severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2 (SARS-CoV-2) circRNA vaccine in mouse and non-human primate immunization studies and demonstrated the induction of protective immune responses in the absence of adverse events[268](#R268). Unfortunately, the report did not include non-replicating mRNA vaccine comparators in multiple experiments, making direct comparisons between the platforms difficult. The most critical limitations of the widespread use of circRNA are the production issues (low RNA circularization efficiency) and the lack of human immunogenicity and safety data (currently used circRNA does not contain modified nucleosides and, thus, it may induce suboptimal high levels of inflammation).

#### Non-replicating mRNA vaccines.

Non-replicating (linear) IVT mRNA has been tested in vaccine studies for decades and can be made with or without the use of modified nucleosides. The unmodified format was primarily pioneered by CureVac AG and it proved to be an effective infectious disease mRNA vaccine format in preclinical studies[16](#R16)–[18](#R18). However, the platform did not perform as well as its nucleoside-modified (*N*1-methylpseudouridine-containing) counterparts in COVID-19 mRNA vaccine clinical trials and was not licensed for humans[19](#R19)–[21](#R21). One potential explanation for the suboptimal performance of the unmodified CureVac COVID-19 vaccine (CVnCoV) was its highly inflammatory nature even at a relatively low (12.5 μg) dose in humans[19](#R19). This higher reactogenicity of the unmodified mRNA prevented testing of high CVnCoV mRNA doses of 30 μg and 100 μg (as utilized in the Pfizer–BioNTech (BNT162b2) and Moderna (mRNA-1273) formulations, respectively), and the 12.5 μg dose did not induce sufficiently robust protective immune responses in a phase IIb–III trial[19](#R19). In groundbreaking studies, the 2023 Nobel Laureates Karikó and Weissman demonstrated that the introduction of certain modified nucleosides into the mRNA sequence increases protein production from the IVT mRNA and, importantly, decreases its innate immune sensing[22](#R22),[23](#R23). This latter feature of nucleoside-modified mRNA enabled BNT162b2 and mRNA-1273 to be used at sufficiently high doses to induce protection against COVID-19 while keeping vaccine-induced adverse events at an acceptable level.

Key considerations for IVT mRNA are its half-life, stability, translatability and inflammatory capacity; features that impact mRNA vaccine efficacy and safety and can be fine-tuned via the optimization of the structural elements (5′ cap, 5′-untranslated region (UTR), coding region, 3′-UTR, poly(A) tail) of the mRNA and/or by utilizing appropriate mRNA chemistry and production procedures[24](#R24)–[27](#R27). The 5′ cap and poly(A) tail both contribute to the stability of the mRNA and play a key role in translation initiation[25](#R25). As of now, cap1 (m7GpppNm) is the most widely used modification that can be added to the IVT mRNA co-transcriptionally using a cap1 analogue or post-transcriptionally using viral capping enzymes[28](#R28),[29](#R29). The use of cap1 instead of cap0 (m7GpppN) increases protein production by overcoming interferon-induced protein with tetratricopeptide repeats 1 (IFIT1)-induced translation inhibition[30](#R30). UTRs from mammalian or viral sequences have also been included for many years to increase mRNA stability and translation[25](#R25). More recently, high-throughput reporter assays and computer-guided approaches have enabled the selection of optimized UTRs and codon compositions for higher mRNA stability and translation[31](#R31)–[36](#R36). Increasing the guanine and cytosine content of genes is generally coupled with a higher level of expression[37](#R37), but recent findings reveal that mRNA secondary structures are also critical predictors of mRNA stability and translatability. Interestingly, the most highly structured mRNAs often (but not always) have the highest guanine and cytosine content, and the codon usage and secondary structures distinctly regulate the translational capacity of IVT mRNA by enhancing in-solution stability[34](#R34)–[36](#R36). The introduction of certain modified nucleosides, such as pseudouridine or *N*1-methylpseudouridine, into the IVT mRNA can increase in-solution stability via the suppression of mRNA hydrolysis at the substituted residues[34](#R34). *N*1-methylpseudouridine substitutions promote decreased phosphorylation of eukaryotic translation initiation factor 2A (eIF2a), thereby increasing protein production from the mRNA[38](#R38). These findings explain, at least in part, why increased protein production from pseudouridine-containing IVT mRNA compared to its unmodified counterpart was found in early studies[23](#R23).

Mitigating the immune-activating capacity of IVT mRNA is key for the development of safe infectious disease vaccines. This can be achieved by at least four approaches. First, the replacement of uridine with pseudouridine or its derivative, *N1*-methylpseudouridine, in the mRNA sequence decreases uridine-dependent immune sensing of IVT mRNA[22](#R22),[39](#R39)–[41](#R41). Second, RNA polymerase-generated short abortive transcripts can form strongly immunostimulatory double-stranded RNAs (dsRNAs) in the IVT mRNA preparations that can induce inflammation and translational blockade via recognition by multiple sensors (for example, TLR3, OAS, PKR, RIG-I and MDA5) in various cellular compartments[27](#R27). Therefore, removing dsRNA contaminants from IVT mRNA preparations through purification or RNase III treatment further increases its translatability and decreases inflammatory capacity[42](#R42)–[44](#R44). The development of an RNA polymerase that produces less dsRNA during transcription potentially allows for the generation of IVT mRNA in a ‘single pot’ reaction, simplifying vaccine manufacturing[45](#R45). Third, cap0 is recognized by the intracellular RNA sensor, RIG-I, which induces inflammatory responses[46](#R46). Therefore, using a 5′ cap1 containing the methyl group on the first cap-proximal nucleotide (a signature of ‘self’ in higher-order eukaryotes) is key to diminishing IVT mRNA-induced immune recognition. Fourth, certain codon optimization strategies increase dsRNA formation within the IVT mRNA[36](#R36) and, therefore, likely increase its recognition by cellular dsRNA sensors. Although many of the above-mentioned considerations for optimal IVT mRNA production have been examined in a series of in vitro and in vivo experiments, sufficient detail about their effect on vaccine immunogenicity in preclinical models is lacking[47](#R47).

#### Self-amplifying mRNA vaccines.

saRNA vaccines using IVT mRNA have been studied for more than a decade. This modality typically uses sequences derived from alphaviruses in which the non-structural proteins (the replicase genes that encode the RNA-dependent RNA polymerase) are intact and the structural proteins are replaced with the antigen of interest[15](#R15). saRNA vaccines could potentially be given to humans at lower doses compared to non-replicating mRNA vaccines to induce protective immune responses, reducing vaccine-induced adverse events and cost. Similarly to non-replicating mRNA, saRNA also contains 5′ cap, 5′-UTR, coding region, 3′-UTR and a poly(A) tail ([Fig. 1](#F1)); therefore, viral backbone-specific optimization of these elements could yield improved vaccine designs. Following saRNA uptake, the viral replicase generates multiple copies of the antigen-encoding RNA, leading to sustained antigen production. During the RNA self-amplification process, dsRNA intermediates are typically produced that elicit robust immune activation, specifically, type I interferon responses via the activation of various cellular RNA sensors[15](#R15). The robust inflammatory response is beneficial for immune cell activation but limits protein production from the saRNA and decreases tolerability. saRNA vaccines showed early promise in preclinical models, inducing robust antibody and T cell responses even at low vaccine doses[48](#R48). Conversely, they did not perform well in humans in early COVID-19 vaccine trials[49](#R49),[50](#R50). However, a recent clinical study reported non-inferiority of the ARCT-154 (Arcturus) COVID-19 saRNA-LNP vaccine compared to the BNT162b2 nucleoside-modified mRNA formulation, used as a booster dose in individuals previously vaccinated three times with any of the two approved COVID-19 mRNA vaccines[51](#R51). Adverse events induced by ARCT-154 and BNT162b2 were comparable. Of note, ARCT-154 was used at a much lower dose (5 μg) compared to BNT162b2 (30 μg). As a result of these promising findings, ARCT-154 was licensed in Japan and became the third mRNA-based drug to gain approval[52](#R52).

saRNAs are produced with conventional unmodified nucleosides due to incompatibility concerns, but a recent seminal publication reported that complete substitution of certain unmodified nucleosides with modified nucleosides results in functional saRNAs with increased translational capacity and significantly decreased inflammatory capacity[53](#R53). Specifically, saRNAs with 5-hydroxymethylcytidine, 5-methylcytidine and 5-methyluridine modifications outperformed their *N*1-methylpseudouridine-modified linear mRNA counterpart in cell transfection efficiency in vitro. Most importantly, when used at a low dose (10 ng RNA), a 5-methylcytidine-containing SARS-CoV-2 saRNA-LNP vaccine induced higher spike antigen-specific IgG titres and a higher level of protection compared to the *N*1-methylpseudouridine-modified linear mRNA-LNP vaccine in mice. If these findings translate to humans, this may open new avenues in the field of saRNA vaccines. Recent research efforts with saRNA vaccines yielded innovative bipartite vector systems in which the antigen of interest and the replicase are encoded on separate mRNAs[54](#R54),[55](#R55) ([Fig. 1](#F1)). The split vector systems are favourable because the length of the saRNAs can be decreased, mitigating the issue of RNA instability, simplifying vaccine production and allowing for individual optimization of both components of the bipartite platform[56](#R56). Notably, a challenge associated with the successful use of this modality is that both RNA components should be present in the same cell, otherwise the vector system will not work. So far, the two-component saRNA modalities have only been tested in mice; therefore, studies in higher-order species are warranted.

### Delivery materials for mRNA vaccines

The topic of mRNA vaccine delivery has been recently reviewed[24](#R24),[57](#R57); therefore, only the most critical considerations and novel recent findings are discussed below.

As mRNA is a large, negatively charged molecule, its passive transfer through cell membranes is an inefficient process. Early mRNA vaccine studies utilized physical methods (for example, gene gun and electroporation), protamine or polymer-complexed mRNA, or administration of RNA-loaded dendritic cells to induce protective immunity[26](#R26). Although these approaches showed some promise in preclinical studies, they did not demonstrate sufficient efficacy and/or proved to be impractical in more advanced preclinical and human trials. Permanently positively charged lipids have been used for in vitro mRNA delivery for decades but they are mostly suboptimal for in vivo use due to the extensive interactions with negatively charged serum proteins; however, this was mainly investigated in the context of intravenous nanoparticle delivery that may have limited relevance for intramuscularly administered infectious disease mRNA vaccines[58](#R58). The delivery problem was solved in the 2010s by the development of LNPs containing ‘ionizable’ lipids[48](#R48),[59](#R59),[60](#R60), which are usually composed of four components: phospholipid (helper lipid), cholesterol, polyethylene glycol (PEG)-conjugated lipid and a cationic ionizable aminolipid (with a pKa of typically 6.0–6.8), which together with the encapsulated nucleic acid, can form stable particles of typically 50–150 nm. Ionizable lipids are uncharged at physiological pH, which makes them safer compared to the permanently positively charged alternatives. They facilitate cellular uptake of the mRNA and release of the nucleic acid cargo from the endosomes to the cytosol via the disruption of the endosomal membrane after becoming positively charged in the acidifying endosome[58](#R58). In addition, they provide strong adjuvant activity for mRNA vaccines[61](#R61),[62](#R62). This inherent immunostimulatory activity of mRNA-LNP vaccines allows for the induction of robust protective immune responses without the addition of further adjuvant. The discovery of ionizable LNPs as efficient nucleic acid carriers and potent immune adjuvants was a serendipitous and groundbreaking innovation that allowed the development of the successful COVID-19 mRNA vaccines mRNA-1273 and BNT162b2. The success of the nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccine platform lies in the use of a minimally immunogenic mRNA to maximize protein (antigen) production, combined with the ability of LNPs to efficiently deliver cargo and foster the induction of strong antigen-specific immune responses.

The highly inflammatory nature of ionizable LNPs can be considered a double-edged sword: while it is critical for the induction of a strong immune response, it can also limit how they can be administered. Intranasal vaccine administration to induce mucosal immune responses is particularly challenging. However, a study from Moderna recently demonstrated efficacy for an intranasally delivered SARS-CoV-2 mRNA-LNP vaccine in hamsters[63](#R63), suggesting that optimizations may overcome the low tolerability and safety of intranasally administered LNPs. Although ionizable LNPs are the most advanced formulations, other materials are also used in experimental RNA vaccines and may offer more flexibility for the route of administration. A novel nanocarrier, termed a lipid inorganic nanoparticle (LION, a cationic oil-in-water emulsion), was optimized for the delivery of saRNA[64](#R64), and then used in preclinical and clinical studies for mRNA vaccine development ([NCT04844268](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT04844268), [NCT05132907](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05132907))[64](#R64)–[68](#R68).

Cationic polymers form polyplexes with mRNA that can be taken up via endocytosis by the target cells[69](#R69). Early studies used polyethylenimine–saRNA complexes to induce immune responses against influenza virus in mice[70](#R70). As polyethylenimine may display a high level of toxicity[71](#R71), it is not a widely used RNA-delivery material. Dendrimers (hyperbranched polymeric tree-like structures) also demonstrated efficacy in preclinical saRNA vaccine studies against Ebola virus, influenza virus, Zika virus (ZIKV) and *Toxoplasma gondii* but no studies with this platform in large animals or humans have been reported[72](#R72),[73](#R73). A recent seminal study demonstrated that cationic biodegradable polymer nanoparticles (poly(amine-*co*-ester) (PACE)) can be utilized for mucosal (lung) vaccination with a SARS-CoV-2 mRNA vaccine via the intranasal route in rodents to elicit protective immunity[74](#R74). Optimization of the amine-containing end group enhances the endosomal escape of mRNA from the endocytosed particles[75](#R75), and PEG content modifications further increase mRNA delivery of the PACE particles[76](#R76). The platform demonstrated safety as no elevation of liver enzymes or any damage in the lungs were found after PACE-mRNA delivery to mice. Most importantly, the authors demonstrated that intratracheally delivered PACE-mRNA complexes are taken up by epithelial cells and antigen-presenting cells in the lungs of mice, and showed that their SARS-CoV-2 spike-encoding vaccine can induce protective immunity in hamsters. Overall, the novel PACE-mRNA platform will be valuable for the induction of mucosal immune responses that are key to limiting respiratory pathogen spread. Large animal studies and clinical trials will be important to confirm whether the impressive findings in rodents with PACE-mRNA translate to higher-order species.

## Mechanisms of action of mRNA vaccines

At a high level, the mechanisms of action of infectious disease mRNA vaccines are fairly simple: mRNA-LNPs encoding one or more foreign antigens are injected (in most cases) intramuscularly, taken up by various cell types that express the encoded antigens, which are then recognized by the immune system and trigger an immune response. However, many key questions remain unanswered. Which cells are expressing the antigen? How does it get to the lymphoid organs? How are innate immune receptors triggered? What is the role of LNPs in inducing an immune response after mRNA vaccination? These topics have been extensively discussed[27](#R27); thus, only the most critical findings will be briefly summarized below.

### Location of antigen expression and transport

Following intramuscular injection of mRNA-LNPs, the antigen-encoding mRNA can be found in the spleen, lymph nodes and plasma as shown in preclinical models, including non-human primates (NHPs)[77](#R77), and in humans[78](#R78),[79](#R79). The mRNA persists in vivo for several days or longer, depending on the dose and the type of LNP[59](#R59),[77](#R77). More importantly, protein expression from intramuscularly delivered mRNA-LNPs occurs at the site of injection but some protein is also detected in the plasma (blood), liver and, potentially, in other organs, including secondary lymphoid organs. Studies in NHPs have found that many infiltrating immune cells as well as fibroblasts and adipocytes translate protein at the site of injection[77](#R77),[80](#R80),[81](#R81). While uptake of mRNA-LNP and expression of protein in myocytes has been reported in some studies, others have shown absence of involvement of myocytes. Early studies with saRNA identified muscle cells as the main type of cell expressing and presenting antigen[82](#R82). The observed differences could be due to differences in LNP formulation, cellular analysis protocol or mRNA platform (non-replicating mRNA versus saRNA) ([Fig. 2](#F2)). Based on these studies, which were mostly performed in NHPs, it is likely that macrophages and monocytes expressing the vaccine antigen migrate to the draining lymph node (dLN) to present antigen to adaptive immune cells[77](#R77),[80](#R80),[81](#R81); however, other scenarios are possible. Mouse studies using saRNA vaccines reported that bone marrow-derived infiltrating antigen-presenting cells, including dendritic cells, might acquire antigen from transfected muscle cells and then present it to adaptive immune cells, including CD8+ T cells[83](#R83). It is also very likely that LNPs can reach the dLNs by passive transport via the lymphatic system ([Fig. 2](#F2)). The antigen (protein) and mRNA can both be detected in macrophages, monocytes and dendritic cells as well as in histiocytes, reticular cells and endothelial cells in the dLN after intramuscular or intradermal immunization in NHPs[77](#R77),[80](#R80),[81](#R81). The presence of mRNA or antigen was also observed in B cells and T cells in the dLN of NHPs but at low levels[77](#R77),[81](#R81). In humans, the mRNA and/or antigen traffic to the lymph nodes effectively and initiate month-long germinal centre reactions[84](#R84)–[86](#R86).

#### Fig. 2 |. mRNA-LNP vaccine trafficking after intramuscular delivery.

![Fig. 2 |](https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/cee2/13101425/23d070f01c55/nihms-2152818-f0002.jpg)

[Open in a new tab](figure/F2/)

**a**, mRNA and the antigen (expressed from the mRNA) are taken up by infiltrating macrophages, monocytes, dendritic cells, fibroblasts and adjacent adipocytes as well as endothelial cells. To what extent myocytes take up mRNA or express antigen after mRNA vaccination is not entirely clear. **b**, Antigen (likely via migrating macrophages, monocytes and dendritic cells) and mRNA–lipid nanoparticles (LNPs) are then transported to the lymph nodes where macrophages, monocytes, histiocytes, different dendritic cell subsets, and endothelial cells associated with the cortical sinus, medullar sinuses, subcapsular sinus and trabeculae, express the antigen. Low levels of antigen are also expressed by B cells and T cells in the draining lymph nodes (not depicted). Although most vaccine antigen and/or mRNA becomes undetectable within days post immunization, vaccine mRNA has been detected in germinal centres for more than 1 month and germinal centre reactions can be maintained for months in humans. Transiently, mRNA can also be found in plasma, liver, spleen and potentially elsewhere depending on the LNP formulation. The figure is based on findings in both non-human primates and humans[77](#R77),[78](#R78),[80](#R80),[81](#R81).

The location of antigen expression is highly relevant because it may directly impact adaptive immune responses. For example, induction of mucosal secretory IgA in the upper respiratory tract was also observed after intramuscular COVID-19 mRNA vaccination in individuals seropositive for SARS-CoV-2, even though this response was not robust[87](#R87). It is thought that such a response can only be induced if the antigen is present locally on mucosal surfaces[88](#R88). It is possible that small amounts of mRNA and/or antigen are transported to the surfaces of the upper respiratory tract via blood, as supported by studies demonstrating the presence of antigen-encoding mRNA in plasma[77](#R77),[78](#R78).

### Innate sensing of the mRNA and LNP

Innate immune signalling of vaccine components is needed to initiate robust adaptive immune responses. However, a very robust immune activation (particularly type I interferon production) can be detrimental since it can shut down antigen expression from the mRNA and cause side effects. As reviewed above, unmodified and unpurified IVT mRNA can activate a number of immune sensors but modification of mRNA with *N*1-methylpseudouridine and purification to remove dsRNA significantly decreases mRNA sensing[27](#R27),[89](#R89).

Besides the antigen-encoding mRNA, components of the LNP carrier, especially ionizable lipids, also trigger innate immune responses. It was shown that empty, ionizable LNPs with recombinant protein have stronger adjuvant activity than a commercial MF59-like adjuvant used with recombinant protein and that the adjuvanticity was highly dependent on the ionizable lipid[61](#R61). In a separate study, human peripheral blood mononuclear cells treated with LNPs containing the ionizable lipid SM-102 and nucleoside-modified mRNA produced many inflammatory cytokines, including IL-1β, IL-6, TNF, CCL5, VEGFA and G-CSF[62](#R62). IL-1β is of particular interest because it is a key mediator of host defence response to infection or injury, and its release implies inflammasome formation. Although it is not fully understood how the lipids in an LNP trigger innate immune activation, an emerging hypothesis is that the disruption of endosomal membranes may play a role. Endosomal escape is an essential step for mRNA to get into the cytosol and is facilitated by lipids within an LNP. However, more recently, this endosomal damage was shown to also directly trigger inflammation via galectin recognition and binding. Inhibition of galectins or utilization of more biodegradable ionizable lipid structures may offer future strategies to reduce immune activation[90](#R90).

Evaluation of BNT162b2 in mice revealed it to trigger an interferon response via activation of natural killer cells and CD8+ T cells in the dLN and, based on knockout mouse studies, this response was dependent on MDA5 signalling[91](#R91). As both RIG-I and MDA5 signal through the mitochondrial antiviral-signalling protein (MAVS), this finding is in contrast to the reported MAVS-independent immune activation by mRNA-LNPs[61](#R61). A seminal study in humans that evaluated the innate immune response to BNT162b2 found strong activation of innate immunity, including a robust antiviral signature, specifically, upregulation of the type I interferon response and RIG-I-like signalling[92](#R92). Induction of the interferon pathway in humans is consistent with the observed reactogenicity of these vaccines that is thought to be triggered by interferon responses. The partially different findings of these studies may reflect the variability in vaccine preparations (mRNA design and production, different LNPs) and in immune response activation between the mouse model and humans.

## Preclinical and clinical mRNA vaccines

The number of published studies on mRNA vaccines has grown significantly in recent years as a consequence of the success of COVID-19 mRNA vaccines[93](#R93). Preclinical mRNA vaccines have been developed not only against a wide range of viruses but also for certain bacteria and parasites, including ticks, some of which have progressed to clinical studies ([Table 1](#T1)). Below, recent preclinical and clinical work with mRNA vaccines is summarized. As SARS-CoV-2 mRNA vaccines have been extensively reviewed[24](#R24),[93](#R93),[94](#R94), only aspects of COVID-19 vaccine reports that have high clinical relevance will be highlighted ([Box 1](#BX1) and discussed below). Efforts with ‘active’ immunization will be focused upon, whilst the recent findings with mRNA-encoded monoclonal antibodies are summarized in [Box 3](#BX3) and [Table 2](#T2).

### Box 3 |. Passive immunotherapy with mRNA.

Monoclonal antibodies (mAbs) have revolutionized modern medicine, particularly in the fields of cancer immunotherapy and autoimmune or inflammatory diseases, and represent a new promising therapeutic modality for the treatment of infectious diseases. Clinically approved mAbs are available against several pathogens such as respiratory syncytial virus, Zaire Ebola virus, human immunodeficiency virus 1 and severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2 (ref. [269](#R269)). However, the high cost of protein production and purification, as well as the need for systemic administration, significantly limit the use of mAbs in the patient population. Additionally, the long timeline for development makes recombinant proteins suboptimal to respond to pandemic scenarios. mRNA-based passive immunotherapy couples simple and efficient manufacturing with the benefit of potentially longer in vivo activity of the mRNA-encoded mAb due to continuous protein production from the encoding mRNA; these advantages may help to make mAb treatment more widely accessible worldwide.

Preclinical studies have provided proof-of-concept for the use of mAb-encoding mRNA, and some clinical data are now available[270](#R270) ([Table 2](#T2)). The flexibility of mRNA design allows for the use of various mRNA-encoded mAb formats such as full-length IgG (light and heavy chains) or shorter versions such as camelid (heavy-chain-only VH domain) or single-chain antibodies ([Table 2](#T2)). mRNA-encoded mAbs have shown success in both prophylactic and therapeutic settings. Lipid nanoparticle (LNP)-formulated non-replicating mRNAs are usually administered intravenously and use the liver as a bioreactor to produce the encoded mAbs. Intravenous administration allows for the rapid (within 4–24 h) generation of high amounts (even ~200 μg/ml peak serum level) of mAbs with a gradual decrease over time[248](#R248). Intramuscular or subcutaneous delivery of mAb-encoding mRNA could be a convenient way of quickly responding to pathogen outbreaks owing to the ease of drug administration. Providing proof-of-concept, a self-amplifying mRNA–lipid inorganic nanoparticle construct given intramuscularly was sufficient to induce the production of a protective level of mAb against Zika virus infection in mice[254](#R254).

Importantly, the requirements of mRNA-based mAb products are different from those of mRNA vaccines: sustained protein production from the mRNA should be coupled with no or minimal inflammation for the development of safe and effective drugs for passive immunotherapy. Using minimally immunogenic platforms is critical given that much higher mRNA doses are often administered for mAb therapy compared to active immunization. The use of modified nucleosides is advantageous to decrease innate immune recognition of the mRNA. The adjuvant (inflammatory) activity of currently used LNPs[61](#R61), which is critical when mRNA vaccines are used for active immunization, is detrimental for passive immunotherapy. Therefore, the development of novel LNPs with minimal inflammatory activity or the use of immunologically more inert materials, such as certain polymers, will likely form the basis of future mRNA-based mAb drugs.
