### Influenza virus

Seasonal influenza virus vaccines often demonstrate suboptimal levels of protection; thus, mRNA-based influenza virus vaccines are receiving significant attention. The mRNA-LNP platform technology has two important advantages compared to the widely used egg-produced vaccines. First, mRNA-based seasonal influenza virus vaccines can be rapidly designed and mass produced. Unlike egg-based vaccines, there is no risk of introducing unwanted mutations during the mRNA vaccine production procedure[95](#R95). Additionally, ease of manufacturing and large-scale production can be critical in a pandemic scenario. Multivalent mRNA vaccines (targeting multiple antigens) can induce broad immunity. Early influenza mRNA vaccine studies, often using a single antigen as the vaccine target[95](#R95), demonstrated convincing preclinical findings. Within the past 5 years, next-generation influenza virus mRNA vaccines have used rationally designed antigens and/or multiple antigens to increase the breadth of protective immune responses[3](#R3),[5](#R5),[6](#R6),[8](#R8),[96](#R96) and, importantly, results from clinical trials have been published. Moderna began phase I trials in 2015 using monovalent nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccines encoding haemagglutinin from H10N8 and H7N9 influenza viruses[97](#R97). The vaccines were moderately immunogenic and well tolerated when delivered intramuscularly at 50 μg or 100 μg doses but antibody titres waned fairly quickly. Moderna and other companies have started clinical trials — also using the multivalent approach — in the past several years ([Table 1](#T1)) and some findings have recently been made public. For example, in a phase I–II trial, intramuscular administration of a single dose of mRNA-1010, a quadrivalent seasonal mRNA-LNP vaccine candidate encoding four full-length haemagglutinins (recommended by the WHO for influenza vaccines in the 2020–2021 influenza season), induced higher haemagglutinin inhibition (HAI) titres against the vaccine-matched influenza A virus strains and comparable titres against the influenza B virus strains than inactivated seasonal influenza vaccine at all tested doses (20 μg, 50 μg and 100 μg)[98](#R98). Interestingly, interim results from the phase III trial with mRNA-1010 were less encouraging; the mRNA vaccine induced more adverse events than the seasonal influenza virus vaccine (70% versus 48%, respectively) and demonstrated similar immunogenicity against the influenza A virus and inferior immunogenicity against the influenza B virus strains[99](#R99). These results suggest that improved mRNA platform technologies and/or rationally designed immunogens may be needed to induce immunity superior to that induced by currently licensed influenza virus vaccines. Moreover, the addition of other antigens such as neuraminidase may help to increase the efficacy and breadth of elicited immunity. Neuraminidase immunogens are included in mRNA-1020 and mRNA-1030 (Moderna) and in several other mRNA vaccines that are being tested in human trials ([Table 1](#T1)). Finally, it is worth mentioning that mRNA-LNP vaccine trials (for example, [NCT05375838](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05375838), [NCT05596734](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05596734), [NCT05585632](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05585632)) using combined formulations, targeting not only influenza virus but also SARS-CoV-2 and RSV, are under way and show promise in humans ([Table 1](#T1)).

### Respiratory syncytial virus

RSV causes serious respiratory disease in infants, young children, elderly and immunocompromised individuals[100](#R100). A major roadblock facing RSV vaccine development was the inability to induce robust neutralizing antibodies (nAbs) against the fusion (F) glycoprotein of RSV that mediates the fusion of the viral and target cell membranes[101](#R101). With a better understanding of the atomic structure, conformational changes and immunogenicity of RSV F, it was possible to rationally design antigens that target the prefusion conformation of F and induce robust nAb responses[101](#R101). Prefusion F protein-based vaccines for older adults and pregnant women (to protect newborns) were approved by the FDA in 2023 (ref. [102](#R102)). Moderna has several RSV mRNA-LNP vaccine candidates in clinical development ([Table 1](#T1)) and some results have been published. After successful preclinical evaluation of mRNA-1777 (ref. [103](#R103)), a prefusion-stabilized RSV F-encoding nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccine, it was evaluated in a phase I clinical study[104](#R104). The vaccine was well tolerated after a single intramuscular injection at all vaccine doses (25–200 μg in adults 18–49 years of age and 25–300 μg in adults 60–79 years of age), and induced nAbs against both RSV A and B types in a dose-dependent manner. Additionally, mRNA-1777 elicited measurable antigen-specific CD4+ T cell responses. An improved version called mRNA-1345 was tested in a large phase III trial with 35,541 participants at or above the age of 60 years[105](#R105). Overall, the vaccine was well tolerated after a single dose of 50 μg mRNA-LNP and demonstrated 83.7% efficacy against RSV-associated lower respiratory tract disease with at least two signs or symptoms and 82.4% efficacy against the disease with at least three signs or symptoms. Its efficacy was 68.4% against RSV-associated acute respiratory disease. Importantly, mRNA-1345 (under the name mRESVIA) has recently received FDA approval and has become the second approved product of Moderna[106](#R106). The vaccine is currently being tested in phase I trials in children aged 5–24 months ([NCT05743881](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05743881)) and in RSV-seropositive children aged 12–59 months ([NCT04528719](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT04528719)).

### Other respiratory viruses

Human metapneumovirus (hMPV) and parainfluenza virus (PIV) both cause respiratory disease, primarily in infants, young children, the elderly and immunocompromised individuals, and no licensed vaccines are available. Moderna has developed a bivalent vaccine candidate, mRNA-1653, which encodes the full-length membrane-bound fusion proteins of hMPV and PIV3. In a phase I study ([NCT03392389](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03392389)), two intramuscular immunizations (given 4 weeks apart) with relatively high vaccine doses (75 μg, 150 μg and 300 μg) induced robust nAb responses against both vaccine targets[107](#R107). Interestingly, similar nAb titres were detected after the administration of higher vaccine doses but adverse events were dose dependent. nAb titres against hMPV A and B remained above the baseline 13 months after administration of the first vaccine dose in most prime-boost dose groups, whilst this was observed only in the 300 μg group against PIV3. Another trial with mRNA-1653 ([NCT04144348](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT04144348)), including adults and children aged 12–59 months, was also conducted but the results were not published.

Overall, the mRNA-LNP platform can be successfully used for targeting respiratory viruses as such vaccines can be quickly developed and produced and — based on significant clinical experience ([Table 1](#T1)) — they are generally well tolerated, can induce robust nAb responses, and can be adjusted to target rapidly changing viruses (if needed). However, several limitations, such as the lack of sufficient Ab durability or protective mucosal immunity in humans, need to be overcome.

### Human immunodeficiency virus 1

HIV-1 infection affects tens of millions of people, and ~1.5 million new infections are registered annually[108](#R108). HIV-1 is one of the most challenging vaccine targets because of its genetic diversity, complex mechanisms of immune evasion and the poor immunogenicity of the envelope (Env) glycoprotein, the sole target of anti-HIV-1 nAbs[109](#R109). Therefore, even after 40 years of active research, no HIV-1 vaccine is available for humans. Recently published studies using mRNA-LNP-encoded rationally designed immunogens have shown some promise in various animal models[110](#R110)–[115](#R115). These reports demonstrated the elicitation of polyfunctional T cell responses against conserved regions of the HIV-1 proteome in mice using mosaic immunogens[112](#R112), the induction of somatic hypermutation to broadly neutralizing antibody lineages in transgenic mice using an saRNA-encoded nanoparticle immunogen[110](#R110), and the generation of polyfunctional antibodies in NHPs using various engineered Env mRNA-LNPs[114](#R114). Further, studies in transgenic mouse models demonstrated that ferritin nanoparticle mRNA-LNP vaccines could induce antibodies with the ability to neutralize heterologous HIV-1 isolates[113](#R113), and germline antibody-targeting mRNA-LNP-encoded immunogens successfully primed broadly neutralizing antibody precursors[111](#R111). A seminal NHP study demonstrated that an mRNA-LNP vaccine co-expressing membrane-bound Env and simian immunodeficiency virus gag proteins, producing virus-like particles, stimulated nAbs against a tier-2 heterologous simian–human immunodeficiency virus[115](#R115).

Overall, the promising preclinical findings with rationally designed immunogens pave the way for clinical development. Indeed, Moderna has ongoing phase I clinical studies ([NCT05001373](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05001373)) evaluating the nanoparticle immunogen mentioned above (mRNA-1644)[110](#R110) encoded as mRNA-LNP along with a second nanoparticle construct, mRNA-1644v2-Core. A second trial ([NCT05217641](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05217641)) uses mRNA-encoded (mRNA-1574) rationally designed Env trimers[116](#R116) in healthy individuals ([Table 1](#T1)), and the US National Institutes of Health also started a phase I study ([NCT05903339](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05903339)) utilizing mRNA-encoded ferritin nanoparticle-based immunogens.

### Flaviviruses

Flaviviruses represent a diverse family of primarily arthropod-transmitted pathogens with significant epidemic potential. Licensed vaccines, using live-attenuated viruses, chemically inactivated viruses or viral vectors, have been developed against certain types of flaviviruses such as Japanese encephalitis virus, tick-borne encephalitis virus, yellow fever virus and dengue virus (DENV)[117](#R117). Human vaccines also need to be developed against other members of flaviviruses that can potentially quickly spread in the human population[118](#R118). Novel vaccines would need to address some of the shortcomings of existing vaccines such as the lack of breadth of protective immunity (DENV) or the potential for inducing adverse events such as antibody-dependent enhancement. A multicomponent vaccine that could induce protective immunity against several flaviviruses would also be highly desirable. Vaccine durability is also key since waning levels of nAbs can enhance disease.

Interestingly, the first published breakthrough studies (in 2017) using nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccines demonstrated the ability of the platform to induce protective immune responses against ZIKV in mice and NHPs[119](#R119),[120](#R120). In subsequent years, mRNA or saRNA-based platforms against flavivirus antigens showed promise in preclinical studies against a series of targets such as ZIKV, DENV, Powassan virus and yellow fever virus[121](#R121),[122](#R122). Moderna developed first-generation and second-generation ZIKV mRNA-LNP vaccines, termed mRNA-1325 and mRNA-1893, respectively. Both vaccines encode the pre-membrane and Env glycoproteins of ZIKV, they only differ in five amino acids in the coding sequence and use different leader sequences[123](#R123). mRNA-1893 elicited comparable nAb titres to mRNA-1325 at 1/20th of the dose, likely due to the ability of mRNA-1893 to form virus-like particles, and provided complete protection from ZIKV challenge in NHPs. Both vaccines were tested in a prime-boost setting (4 weeks between the two injections) in phase I clinical trials ([NCT03014089](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03014089) and [NCT04064905](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT04064905)) in flavivirus seronegative or seropositive healthy individuals[124](#R124). In general, findings of these trials were similar to other phase I safety and immunogenicity mRNA vaccine trials; the vaccines were well tolerated in a wide dose range (10–250 μg) and more adverse events were found at higher doses and after the second immunization. While mRNA-1325 induced poor nAb responses, mRNA-1893 performed well and all doses induced long-lived (>13 months post prime) nAb responses in both flavivirus seronegative and seropositive individuals.

### Other viruses

mRNA vaccine candidates have been developed against multiple other relevant human pathogenic viruses.

Several families of RNA viruses have members that can cause viral haemorrhagic fever in humans, such as arenaviruses, bunyaviruses, filoviruses and flaviviruses, and no vaccines are available against most of these pathogens. While most haemorrhagic fever viruses (HFVs) are considered neglected tropical diseases, the combined public health impact of all of these pathogens is significant. Larger outbreaks, as seen with Ebola virus in West Africa in 2014–2016, and potential pandemics are of major concern. Because some of the potential vaccine antigens (for example, bunyavirus glycoproteins) in these viral groups have complex folding and are hard to express using standard methods, the mRNA platform is well suited for vaccine development against these pathogens. Although two viral vector-based vaccines are available against one of the most relevant Ebola viruses, Zaire Ebola virus, concerns about the safety profile of the vectored platforms[125](#R125) justify the development of novel Ebola virus vaccines using alternative technologies. Both self-replicating and non-replicative mRNA vaccines targeting the viral glycoprotein of Ebola virus induced protective immunity in rodents[73](#R73),[126](#R126), warranting future studies in large animals and, potentially, in humans. Similarly promising results were obtained with mRNA vaccines against other HFVs, such as Lassa virus[127](#R127), Crimean–Congo HFV[67](#R67),[128](#R128) and severe fever with thrombocytopenia syndrome virus[129](#R129), in rodent vaccine studies.

mRNA vaccine candidates have been developed against other viruses with pandemic potential such as Nipah virus and mpox virus (MPXV). Two preclinical Nipah studies — targeting either the soluble Hendra virus glycoprotein or the prefusion stabilized fusion protein covalently linked to the glycoprotein monomer of Nipah virus — demonstrated protective immunity in Syrian hamsters and mice, respectively[130](#R130),[131](#R131). The latter vaccine candidate (mRNA-1215) is being tested in a phase I clinical trial ([NCT05398796](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05398796)) by Moderna. mRNA vaccines were quickly generated and tested against MPXV in response to the 2022 virus outbreak. Moderna developed a vaccine candidate targeting four highly conserved MPXV surface proteins (M1, A29, A35 and B6) involved in virus attachment, and demonstrated protection from infection with the related vaccinia virus (VACV) in mice[132](#R132). The company has started a phase I trial ([NCT05995275](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05995275)) with its MPXV mRNA vaccine candidate, mRNA-1769. A similar approach — targeting viral fusion proteins — demonstrated protective immunity from VACV challenge in mice[133](#R133). BioNTech also developed MPXV vaccine candidates, the quadrivalent BNT166a targeting M1, A35, B6, and H3, and a trivalent vaccine without the H3 component, BNT166c[134](#R134). Both vaccines protected mice from VACV, clade I and clade IIb MPXV infection, and BNT166a protected NHPs from lethal clade I MPXV infection. BNT166a has entered a clinical trial ([NCT05988203](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05988203)).

Licensed vaccines against rabies virus, using the inactivated pathogen, are available and have saved numerous lives over the past decades. However, limitations of the existing formulations include a relatively high cost of production and the need for multiple doses in endemic areas. Multiple mRNA vaccine candidates, targeting the viral glycoprotein, have shown promise in small and large animals[16](#R16),[135](#R135),[136](#R136). CureVac tested its unmodified mRNA-LNP vaccine candidate, CV7202 (ref. [137](#R137)), in a phase I trial ([NCT03713086](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03713086)) and found that the vaccine induced nAbs in humans but only low vaccine doses (1 μg and 2 μg) were well tolerated in a prime-boost schedule. Switching to the nucleoside-modified version of this vaccine would likely allow the use of higher vaccine doses to increase the elicited immune responses without inducing more adverse events.

Human cytomegalovirus (HCMV) causes common congenital infections and is particularly dangerous in immunocompromised individuals. Significant effort has been invested in the development of safe and effective vaccines against HCMV. Several preclinical mRNA vaccine candidates use nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP to target the viral fusion glycoprotein B and/or the pentameric complex necessary for entry and induced nAbs and cellular immune responses in mice, rabbits and NHPs[4](#R4),[138](#R138)–[140](#R140). Moderna tested mRNA-1647 (a multivalent HCMV mRNA-LNP vaccine targeting glycoprotein B and the pentameric complex), administered intramuscularly at months 0, 2 and 6 at a dose of 180 μg, in a phase I trial[141](#R141), finding polyfunctional and durable HCMV-specific antibody responses in both seronegative and seropositive trial participants, with lower glycoprotein B-specific IgG responses but higher overall nAb and antibody-dependent cellular cytotoxicity responses than the comparator, an MF59-adjuvanted glycoprotein B protein vaccine.

Nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccine development against genital herpes — a very common sexually transmitted disease caused by herpes simplex virus type 1 (HSV-1) and HSV-2 — has also been reported[142](#R142)–[145](#R145). An ideal anti-HSV vaccine would prevent painful genital lesions and reduce or eliminate viral shedding, which risks transmission to partners and newborns. A trivalent HSV-2 mRNA-LNP vaccine targeting the entry molecule glycoprotein gD2 and two immune evasion molecules, glycoproteins gC2 and gE2 (ref. [142](#R142)), protected mice and guinea pigs from developing genital lesions and reduced viral shedding. In follow-up studies, the vaccine protected mice from HSV-1 genital lesions and latent infection[144](#R144), and provided protection for neonatal mice[145](#R145). The formulation, named BNT163, is currently being tested in a phase I dose escalation, immunogenicity and safety trial by BioNTech ([NCT05432583](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05432583)). Moderna is also running a phase I trial ([NCT06033261](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT06033261)) with an HSV-2 mRNA-LNP candidate, mRNA-1608, used as an intramuscularly administered prime-boost vaccine.

Several other promising mRNA vaccine candidates targeting important human viral pathogens are in preclinical or clinical development with limited or no available published data such as candidate formulations against hepatitis B virus[146](#R146), hepatitis C virus[147](#R147), Epstein–Barr virus (EBV)[148](#R148), varicella-zoster virus[149](#R149),[150](#R150) and norovirus. Moderna is investigating the clinical EBV vaccine candidates mRNA-1189 (targeting gp42, gp220, gH and gL glycoproteins, [NCT05164094](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05164094)) and mRNA-1195 ([NCT05831111](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05831111)) in phase I trials to prevent infectious mononucleosis and rare but sometimes life-threatening consequences of EBV infection or long-term sequelae such as multiple sclerosis[151](#R151). Moderna is also studying the mRNA vaccines mRNA-1403 (trivalent) and mRNA-1405 (pentavalent) against norovirus infections in phase I trials ([NCT05992935](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05992935)). Finally, both Moderna (mRNA-1468, targeting glycoprotein E) and BioNTech–Pfizer (BNT167, targeting glycoprotein E) have clinical candidate vaccines against varicella-zoster virus in phase I ([NCT05701800](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05701800) and [NCT05703607](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05703607)).

### Bacteria

Given the rise of antimicrobial resistance in many human pathogenic bacteria, the development of safe and effective bacterial vaccines is warranted. However, vaccine development is often more challenging against bacteria than against viruses because of the high number of potential vaccine targets (bacterial genomes are usually much bigger than viral genomes), non-protein vaccine targets (sugars; mRNA vaccines cannot be utilized to target carbohydrates) and high antigenic variation[152](#R152). Additionally, the desired vaccine-induced immune responses are often qualitatively different for bacterial vaccines compared to viral vaccines. Induction of nAbs is often sufficient for protection against viruses but extracellular bacteria (for example, *Streptococcus pneumoniae, Escherichia coli, Staphylococcus aureus* and others) are effectively targeted by immune mechanisms such as complement-mediated killing and antibody-mediated phagocytosis[153](#R153). Furthermore, while not absolutely required for most viral targets, the induction of robust CD8+ T cell responses is often critical to efficiently target intracellular bacteria[153](#R153).

Studies have indicated that mRNA vaccines might be suitable to target bacteria. Intranasally delivered unmodified naked mRNA has been used to induce immune responses against *Mycobacterium tuberculosis* (tuberculosis)[154](#R154), for which infection affects over one billion people worldwide. The currently available tuberculosis vaccine, bacillus Calmette–Guerin, has low effectivity, poor durability and often induces adverse events[155](#R155); therefore, generating a safer and more effective tuberculosis vaccine is an unmet clinical need. The saRNA–lipid inorganic nanoparticle platform encoding a fusion protein of four tuberculosis antigens (esxV, RpfD, PPE60 and Ag85B) demonstrated some level of protection from *M. tuberculosis* infection in mice[66](#R66). The saRNA platform was also utilized to develop a preclinical vaccine against *Streptococcus* groups A and B[156](#R156).

Recently, a nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccine encoding the engineered version of the circular permutated F1 (cp-caf1) *Yersinia pestis* antigen was shown to induce robust humoral responses and protection against lethal plague infection after administration of a single dose in mice[157](#R157). An elegant immunopeptidomics-based approach was applied to identify ideal vaccine targets and develop mRNA vaccines for *Listeria monocytogenes*, a food-borne intracellular bacterium[158](#R158). By encoding highly presented antigens (selected from multiple bacterial proteins) in mRNA-LNPs, robust CD8+ T cell responses and protective immunity from *Listeria* infection in mouse challenge studies were achieved. *Pseudomonas aeruginosa*, a critical human pathogen with multidrug-resistant strains and a significant cause of ventilator-associated pneumonia, has also been targeted[159](#R159). A combination mRNA-LNP vaccine, containing the fusion protein membrane porin F (OprF)–lipoprotein I (OprI) and the V-antigen (PcrV; part of the *P. aeruginosa* type I secretion system), induced protective immune responses in a systemic infection mouse model. In addition, an mRNA-LNP vaccine against Lyme disease (the most common vector-borne disease in the USA), developed using the outer surface protein (OspA) of *Borrelia burdorferi* as a vaccine target, demonstrated induction of robust cellular and humoral immune responses and protection from bacterial challenge in mice[160](#R160). mRNA-LNP vaccines targeting tick salivary proteins to prevent tick-borne diseases, such as Lyme disease, have also been studied in rodents ,[161](#R161),[162](#R162). Moderna and BioNTech are conducting clinical trials to develop mRNA vaccines against Lyme disease and tuberculosis, respectively. mRNA-1975 (a heptavalent formulation) and mRNA-1982 (a monovalent vaccine), both by Moderna, target OspA and are in phase I–II development ([NCT05975099](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05975099)). BNT164a1 and BNT164b1 are tuberculosis vaccine candidates by BioNTech that are in a phase I trial ([NCT05547464](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05547464)).

### Parasites

Pathogenic parasites are a diverse group of eukaryotes affecting hundreds of millions of people and causing more than 1 million deaths every year[163](#R163). However, vaccine development is notoriously difficult against these pathogens, mainly due to the complexity of eukaryotic cells as vaccine targets and their ability to escape from innate and adaptive sterilizing immune responses. A growing number of preclinical studies have demonstrated the ability of mRNA vaccines to provide protection against parasitic diseases. For example, protective preclinical mRNA vaccines were developed against the obligate intracellular parasite *T. gondii*, the causative agent of toxoplasmosis, and against *Leishmania*, the protozoan causative agent of leishmaniasis[73](#R73),[164](#R164). mRNA vaccine candidates against malaria have also been generated and have entered clinical development.

Malaria infections represent one of the world’s leading health problems, responsible for hundreds of millions of clinical cases and hundreds of thousands of deaths every year[165](#R165). Malaria vaccines can be divided into three categories: pre-erythrocytic vaccines that target sporozoites or liver-stage parasites, blood-stage vaccines that aim at merozoites or infected erythrocytes, and transmission-blocking vaccines that target gametocytes and mosquito-stage parasites[166](#R166). An adjuvanted protein-based pre-erythrocytic malaria vaccine targeting the circumsporozoite protein (CSP) of *Plasmodium falciparum* (PfCSP) was approved for use in humans but, while it clearly provides public health benefit, there is still room to improve its effectiveness[166](#R166). In recent years, multiple preclinical studies have provided proof-of-concept for the ability of nucleoside-modified mRNA vaccines to elicit protection from malaria, caused by *P. falciparum* and *Plasmodium vivax*. The vaccine antigens included PfCSP[167](#R167),[168](#R168), the glutamic acid-rich protein of *P. falciparum* (PfGARP)[169](#R169), and the *P. falciparum* ookinete surface (Pfs) 25 and 230D1 proteins[167](#R167),[170](#R170) targeting the *P. falciparum* pathogen at the pre-erythrocytic, blood and transmission stages, respectively. Importantly, clinical development of an mRNA-encoded PfCSP vaccine candidate, BNT165b1, was started in 2022 by BioNTech ([NCT05581641](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05581641)).

An saRNA vaccine encoding the *Plasmodium* macrophage migration inhibitory factor (PMIF), which modulates the host inflammatory response to malaria[171](#R171), improved control of liver-stage and blood-stage *Plasmodium* infection and provided complete protection from re-infection in a lethal murine model. In a mechanistic study, intravenous administration of a modified natural killer T cell agonist α-galactosylceramide-adjuvanted lipoplex-based mRNA vaccine encoding the ribosomal protein L6 (RPL6) malaria antigen elicited liver-resident memory T cells and induced protection in mice[172](#R172).

While *P. falciparum* malaria is responsible for more deaths compared to *P. vivax*, the latter affects many more (over 3 billion) people worldwide and there is currently no approved vaccine[173](#R173). *P. vivax* malaria is also harder to control and eliminate because of its hypnozoite liver stage, which lies dormant for months to years before reactivation[174](#R174). A recent mouse study developed transmission-blocking vaccines using Pvs25 as a vaccine antigen and demonstrated that an optimized full-length Pvs25-encoding nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP vaccine candidate outperformed the comparator adjuvanted Pvs25 protein vaccine and elicited stronger and longer-lasting functional immunity[175](#R175).

Overall, there has been significant recent progress in the field of mRNA-based malaria vaccines, providing hope that such vaccines will be available for humans in the foreseeable future.

## Challenges, limitations and opportunities

Although the nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP platform technology proved to be highly effective during the COVID-19 pandemic and is an important part of our armamentarium for pandemic preparedness, the current generation of mRNA vaccines has limitations that need to be overcome to further increase the value of this vaccine modality. Of note, many of these challenges are not specific to mRNA vaccines and are applicable to other vaccine platforms as well.

### Limitations of preclinical models

#### Immunogenicity.

A general problem for vaccine development, including mRNA-LNP vaccines, is the suboptimal predictive value of preclinical models for immunogenicity in humans. A stark example is an H10 influenza virus vaccine candidate developed by Moderna. The haemagglutinin-based H10 vaccine induced very high HAI titres in ferrets, reaching ~1:3,000, which is typically very hard to induce with traditional vaccine platforms[97](#R97),[176](#R176). The same vaccine induced a transient, approximately 1:100, HAI titre in humans that waned within 6 months[97](#R97),[176](#R176). Similarly, mRNA-LNP-based seasonal influenza virus vaccines induce very high neutralizing antibody titres in different animal models[177](#R177) but seem comparable — and in some cases inferior — to regular seasonal influenza virus vaccines (which have a better tolerability) in humans[98](#R98). Similar findings were reported for COVID-19 mRNA-LNP vaccines; they induce strong and long-lasting immune responses in mice[178](#R178) but less impressive immune responses and kinetics in humans[179](#R179). NHP models seem to better reflect vaccine-induced immune responses in humans; however, they are expensive, their use raises ethical concerns and, in some cases, vaccine-induced protection cannot be assessed since NHPs are refractory to some infections (including seasonal influenza viruses).

#### Safety and reactogenicity.

In humans, mRNA-LNP vaccines trigger systemic inflammatory responses that were not predicted by studies in mice and other animal models. This finding is at least partially due to the interspecies differences in IL-1 receptor signalling[62](#R62). These adverse events (termed reactogenicity) are sometimes relatively strong and include fever, malaise, headache, body aches, and others. Reactogenicity is more prevalent in younger individuals[180](#R180) and is often stronger after administration of the second mRNA vaccine dose[180](#R180), suggesting the involvement of not only innate immune responses but also the contribution of adaptive immunity. Interestingly, mRNA-1273 was reported to induce stronger reactogenicity compared to BNT162b2 (ref. [180](#R180)), potentially due to differences in doses, mRNA production procedures and LNP formulations. Vaccine-induced adverse events can be severe enough to lead to lost work hours[181](#R181), which may not only negatively impact the future willingness of individuals to get vaccinated but also causes economic burden. Some very rare side effects, including anaphylactic reactions (also seen with other vaccines)[182](#R182) and myocarditis, which was mostly seen in males between the ages of 12 and 24 years[183](#R183), are more problematic. Delayed large local reactions were also observed with mRNA-1273 (ref. [184](#R184)) and skin rashes have been recently reported in a clinical trial with HIV-1 mRNA-LNP vaccines (Moderna)[185](#R185). To make mRNA-LNP vaccines more acceptable in a routine, non-pandemic setting, the safety and reactogenicity profile of this modality should be improved. Modifying the LNP formulation and improving mRNA design and production may dampen innate immune recognition of mRNA[186](#R186). However, it has to be noted that reactogenicity and immunogenicity often go hand in hand[187](#R187) and vaccines with an improved safety profile may induce weaker immune responses.

A key challenge is the lack of relevant animal models for assessment of vaccine-induced side effects. There are subtle differences in innate immune receptors between species that make it difficult to predict human reactogenicity based on data obtained in animal models. Additionally, pre-existing immunity to many pathogens and various states of immune activation in humans may influence reactogenicity and it is challenging to model these conditions in animals. Identifying and investigating very rare side effects like myocarditis or anaphylactic reactions is even more complicated as their prevalence is often very low (few events per 100,000 administered vaccine doses)[188](#R188). Therefore, it is unlikely that even a phase III clinical trial will be sufficient to gain relevant information in this regard.

### Inducing the desired immune response

#### Mucosal immunity.

Intramuscular or intradermal injected vaccines are typically poor inducers of mucosal immunity in the upper respiratory tract (URT)[189](#R189). However, local immunity in the URT is important to fight against respiratory virus infections as this site is the entry port for these pathogens as well as a site of dissemination ([Fig. 3](#F3)). Early during COVID-19 vaccine development it was noted that injected mRNA vaccines would likely protect well against symptomatic disease in humans, but would likely not protect robustly against infection per se, which was already observed in animal models during preclinical development of these vaccines[190](#R190). It is now clear that this prediction was accurate. While COVID-19 mRNA vaccines can elicit some level of mucosal immunity in individuals who were previously infected with SARS-CoV-2 (ref. [87](#R87)), they typically do not induce robust antibody responses in the URT compared to SARS-CoV-2 infection[191](#R191). This is problematic as mucosal immunity in the URT has been linked to protection from infection with SARS-CoV-2 (refs. [192](#R192)–[194](#R194)) and other respiratory viruses. A key challenge is the formulation of mRNA vaccines that safely induce strong mucosal immune responses in the URT by eliciting secretory IgA and resident memory T cells[195](#R195),[196](#R196) ([Fig. 3](#F3)). One strategy to address this is to develop mRNA formulations that can be safely and efficiently delivered to mucosal surfaces via intranasal or oral administration. Most current LNP-based mRNA formulations are suboptimal for this delivery route due to poor uptake and toxicity[197](#R197). Other types of mRNA delivery molecules, such as polymers, may be more suitable for this purpose[74](#R74). Indeed, the first successful attempts towards mucosal mRNA vaccine formulations have been made via various changes in delivery, and these promising studies will hopefully provide a path forward for this important vaccination route[63](#R63),[74](#R74),[198](#R198),[199](#R199). A second strategy could be the use of injected mRNA vaccines that encode, in addition to the antigen, factors or even homing molecules that drive B cell and T cell responses to mucosal sites. However, this approach would first require elucidation of homing mechanisms for the URT[200](#R200). In the context of immunity to bacterial infections on mucosal surfaces, co-expression of cytokines or transcription factors that drive T helper 17 T cell responses could also be beneficial.

##### Fig. 3 |. Mucosal immunity, respiratory viruses and mRNA vaccination.

![Fig. 3 |](https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/cee2/13101425/674efc61e3cf/nihms-2152818-f0003.jpg)

[Open in a new tab](figure/F3/)

**a**, The surfaces of the upper respiratory tract serve as the main entry point of respiratory viruses, while virus replication in the lower respiratory tract is often associated with severe disease. Locally produced secretory IgA1 (sIgA1) is thought to be the main antibody species to protect the upper respiratory tract, while serum IgG1 is thought to be the main antibody species to protect the lower respiratory tract. **b**, Intramuscular vaccination with mRNA–lipid nanoparticle vaccines induces robust systemic IgG responses; some of this IgG is transiently also found in the upper respiratory tract. However, local sIgA1 responses are not robustly induced and therefore viral spread is not efficiently blocked. c, Development of mucosal mRNA vaccines, for example, using new delivery vehicles such as polymers or less inflammatory lipid nanoparticles, may allow the safe induction of local antibody and cellular immune responses in the upper respiratory tract. **d**, Co-delivery of mRNA encoding upper respiratory tract-homing molecules expressed in B cells and T cells could potentially elicit efficient mucosal immune responses even after intramuscular mRNA vaccination. Immune responses in parts **c** and **d** could potentially also block onward transmission in case of breakthrough infections.

#### Pre-existing immunity and imprinting.

COVID-19 mRNA-LNP vaccines elicited robust protective immune responses in most immunized individuals[201](#R201). Vaccinating a population that is completely naive is a rare scenario that mostly pertains to the emergence of a new pandemic pathogen. Humans often have extensive and complex pre-existing immunity to pathogens, including influenza virus, RSV and now also SARS-CoV-2, and this pre-existing immunity will influence immune responses to vaccination in both positive and negative ways. In individuals with extensive pre-existing immunity to the SARS-CoV-2 spike protein, additional mRNA vaccine booster doses had a much smaller boosting effect[179](#R179) (these dampening effects of pre-existing antibodies on immune response to vaccination have also been described for influenza[189](#R189)). Furthermore, pre-existing immunity may influence the quality and characteristics of the immune response. SARS-CoV-2 variant-based mRNA vaccines were introduced in 2022 because the virus had antigenically changed. When the updated vaccines were used in individuals who were previously exposed to the ancestral SARS-CoV-2 spike, most of the response was cross-reactive to the spike protein of the ancestral strain of SARS-CoV-2 (refs. [202](#R202)–[206](#R206)), and variant-specific de novo immunity was barely induced[202](#R202)–[206](#R206). This is reminiscent of backboost[207](#R207) or original antigenic sin-type[208](#R208) phenomena described for influenza virus, in which exposure to a new variant virus induces a cross-reactive response that targets the new strain and strains encountered earlier in life at the cost of de novo strain-specific responses to the new strain. While these issues with pre-existing immunity and the lack of robust de novo responses are a general phenomenon and a problem with all vaccine platforms, it would nevertheless be helpful to test mRNA vaccines in animal models with pre-existing immunity to spur their successful deployment. Additionally, since mRNA formulations could also be designed to co-express cytokines and chemokines, the platform may be uniquely suited to develop vaccines that specifically engage naive B cells over memory B cells to drive better de novo variant-specific responses. This would not only be important for SARS-CoV-2 but also for influenza virus vaccines[189](#R189).

#### Longevity of vaccine-induced immune responses.

The longevity of mRNA-LNP vaccine-elicited immune responses has been an important matter of debate. mRNA vaccines for SARS-CoV-2 induce strong plasmablast responses that lead to a significant increase in antibody titres in SARS-CoV-2-naive individuals[84](#R84). Plasmablasts are short-lived B cells that circulate in the periphery and produce antibodies but they are active for only about 2 weeks[209](#R209). The levels of antibodies then start to decrease with an IgG half-life of about 30 days[210](#R210). Due to the high initial mRNA vaccine-induced anti-SARS-CoV-2 antibody levels, this waning appears dramatic, with often 10–20-fold reductions in titres within 6 months after immunization[179](#R179). A slower antibody decay was observed with adenovirus-vectored vaccine platforms[211](#R211); however, those do not induce the initial high antibody peak[211](#R211). In fact, while spike-specific antibody titres induced by COVID-19 mRNA vaccines start to decrease initially, they eventually become stable. These stable, long-term titres are produced by a different subset of B cells in the bone marrow, called long-lived plasma cells, that often persist for life[179](#R179). It has also been observed that mRNA-LNP vaccination elicits surprisingly long (several months) germinal centre reactions in the lymph nodes of vaccinated humans[84](#R84),[86](#R86). These could contribute to an increased frequency of long-lived plasma cells but the significance of these persistent germinal centre reactions is still unclear. Overall, while there is a significant initial waning of mRNA vaccine-induced antibody responses, this may be more of an effect of the initially induced high antibody peak rather than the actual inability of mRNA vaccines to induce long-lasting immunity. However, it would be desirable to maintain high antibody levels after mRNA vaccination to guarantee long-term protection. This may be feasible by optimizing vaccine formulations to direct more B cells from the germinal centre reactions to the bone marrow. For example, a next generation of rationally designed LNPs may allow for the precise targeting of critical immune cell types and, eventually, the induction of the desired immune responses. Other possibilities are the incorporation of molecules with additional adjuvant activity to the LNP to further increase their potency[212](#R212) or the co-expression of transcription activators, cytokines or homing molecules that direct B cells to become long-lived bone marrow plasma cells.

#### CD8+ T cell responses.

While neutralizing and binding antibodies serve as correlates of protection for many viral vaccines, cytotoxic T cell responses may also correlate with protection (for example, for influenza virus[213](#R213)). It is believed that CD8+ T cell responses protect from SARS-CoV-2-elicited progression to severe COVID-19 in cases when the antibody response fails to neutralize the virus[214](#R214) (often seen with antigenically drifted variants). Therefore, another important goal of nucleoside-modified mRNA vaccines is the induction of robust CD8+ T cell responses in humans. Influenza virus nucleoside-modified mRNA vaccines induced robust CD4+ T cell (and antibody) responses but moderate CD8+ T cell responses in mice[215](#R215). These observations held true to some degree with mRNA-based COVID-19 vaccines in humans, where usually weaker spike-specific CD8+ T cell responses are elicited compared to the robust CD4+ T cell induction[216](#R216) (although CD8+ T cells seem to be primed sufficiently to be robustly activated by breakthrough infections)[217](#R217). This is in contrast to unmodified non-replicating mRNA and saRNA vaccines, which are known to drive robust CD8+ T cell responses, likely due to their ability to induce a higher level of type I interferon that helps to induce more robust cellular immune responses[218](#R218). It remains to be explored how CD8+ T cells can be better engaged by nucleoside-modified mRNA vaccines and whether co-expression of specific cytokines or changes in mRNA design and production or in LNP chemistry[219](#R219) could be the key to solving this limitation.

#### Antibody generation against the delivery vehicle.

mRNA-1273 and BNT162b2 induce an adaptive immune response not only against the encoded vaccine antigen but — somewhat surprisingly — also against the LNP carrier. The PEG component on the surface of the LNPs has been identified as the main culprit for off-target antibody responses[220](#R220)–[223](#R223). Anti-PEG antibody levels were induced at a higher level with the mRNA-1273 formulation as compared to the BNT162b2 formulation, likely due to different composition of the vaccine formulations[220](#R220). Although it is not clear if these responses are always detrimental, it is important to further monitor this phenomenon and explore potential consequences. Antibodies to LNPs could potentially influence the uptake of LNPs by removing them or directing them to different Fc receptor-bearing cell types[223](#R223); this could lead to efficiency issues for repeat dosing with the same LNP-based platform. In addition, increased levels of anti-PEG antibodies could potentially result in faster clearance (and therefore lower effectiveness) of other PEGylated drugs that are used in certain patient populations[224](#R224). Taken together, LNPs are not immunologically inert molecules and can be the target of adaptive immune responses. However, alternatives to PEG exist that could potentially have lower immunogenicity.

### Vaccine formulation and selection

#### Multicomponent vaccine formulations.

An interesting aspect of multivalent mRNA-LNP vaccines that has not been thoroughly examined is the potential issue of hetero-multimerization of mRNA-encoded antigens. For example, when two (or more) different SARS-CoV-2 variant spike proteins are expressed by an mRNA vaccine in the same cell, trimers comprised of monomers from different variants may form. A similar issue can potentially occur in mRNA-LNP-based influenza virus vaccines containing four different types of haemagglutinins (H1, H3 and two types of B haemagglutinins). The formation of heterotrimers can potentially lead to misfolding of the resulting proteins that would then potentially not faithfully reflect the neutralizing or protective epitopes on the surface of the glycoproteins. This theoretical concern has not been investigated experimentally but could be one of the reasons for the inferior performance of the ancestral–BA.5 bivalent COVID-19 mRNA vaccine compared to the XBB.1.5 monovalent vaccine[225](#R225), and may also play a role in the lower immunogenicity of the influenza B virus component of clinically tested mRNA-based influenza vaccines[98](#R98). Furthermore, it is possible that one component of a multivalent or multicomponent vaccine exerts immunodominance over other components. This has been described for other vaccine types and could potentially be overcome by adjusting the ratio of various mRNAs in the vaccine, for example, lowering the amount of mRNA encoding the dominant component and/or increasing the amount of the subdominant component.

#### Dose selection.

An important aspect ofthe clinical development of mRNA vaccines is dose selection. Initial SARS-CoV-2 mRNA vaccine clinical trials included doses at or above 100 μg of mRNA[226](#R226),[227](#R227). The first version of mRNA-1273 was dosed at 100 μg of mRNA[20](#R20), but later versions, used for booster doses, had a reduced dose of 50 μg (ref. [228](#R228)), likely to reduce adverse events. The 50 μg mRNA dose seems to be the upper limit for tolerability for current human prophylactic vaccines (however, changing mRNA and LNP chemistry may help to improve it), which represents a limitation for the maximum number of antigen components included in multivalent mRNA-LNP vaccines. For example, octavalent mRNA-LNP vaccine candidates for influenza virus, which include four haemagglutinin and four neuraminidase constructs, are currently in development ([NCT05333289](https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT05333289)). Assuming that the different components are used at the same amount, only 6.25 μg of mRNA per construct can be used in order to not exceed the 50 μg total vaccine dose. It is unclear whether these low doses could induce sufficiently robust and durable immune responses in humans. A 10 μg SARS-CoV-2 mRNA dose induced significantly lower immune responses than the 30 μg dose in a naive population[227](#R227), suggesting that there are valency limitations of mRNA vaccines for clinical use, at least for the current formulations. Overcoming these barriers is critical for the development of pan-respiratory virus vaccines that could contain antigens from influenza virus, RSV, SARS-CoV-2, hMPV and different parainfluenzaviruses as well as seasonal coronaviruses. Simpler multivalent formulations, for example mRNA-LNP vaccines containing influenza virus and SARS-CoV-2 (or even RSV) antigens, are already in development ([Table 1](#T1)).

#### Response to antigenic changes.

A key advantage of mRNA vaccines is their flexibility, especially for vaccines that require frequent updates such as influenza virus vaccines and SARS-CoV-2 vaccines. Inactivated virus-based influenza vaccines undergo annual updates to reflect circulating strains[229](#R229). The components of the vaccine formulation for the coming Northern hemisphere season are selected every February based on surveillance and prediction (a similar process occurs for vaccines made for the Southern hemisphere)[229](#R229). Production of the updated vaccines takes approximately 6 months, during which time it sometimes becomes clear that the selected vaccine strains are not optimal and will likely not match with the circulating strains, having a significant negative impact on vaccine effectiveness[230](#R230),[231](#R231). SARS-CoV-2 mRNA vaccines have been updated and produced within approximately 2 months. Therefore, the use of the mRNA platform for seasonal influenza virus vaccines would potentially allow for a much later strain selection, and enable the development of a vaccine that is better matched to circulating strains than vaccines produced using traditional platforms.

#### Reverse vaccinology 3.0.

When new vaccines move to clinical development, often only one vaccine antigen is tested due to the high costs and complexity of the Good Manufacturing Practice production process that often needs to be specifically designed for the chosen antigen[232](#R232) ([Box 4](#BX4)). This antigen may be tested at different doses or in combination with different adjuvants, but including additional versions of the antigen is often cost-prohibitive. mRNA vaccines overcome this limitation as the same process can be used for manufacturing, even if the antigen sequences differ ([Box 4](#BX4)). mRNA vaccine technology also allows for the targeting of large multisubunit antigens or viral surface antigens that would be hard to produce as recombinant proteins because of the presence of several transmembrane domains (for example, bunyaviral GnGc proteins). Testing of many different versions of an antigen or many different antigens of a pathogen in preclinical trials in parallel with clinical testing of several candidates is also realistic with mRNA vaccines. The optimal vaccine candidates can then be selected based on data obtained in human trials. This process was utilized in the selection process of the Pfizer–BioNTech COVID-19 vaccines, in which BNT162b2 was chosen as the leading candidate after clinical testing of several different versions[227](#R227). In the future, this significant advantage of mRNA vaccines may lead to breakthroughs in development of vaccines against pathogens that express many different proteins and where the optimal vaccine targets are not known, including tuberculosis, different unicellular and multicellular parasites including *Plasmodium* and *Trypanosoma* species, or even DNA viruses with large genomes and unknown protective antigens like African swine fever virus. This ‘reverse vaccinology 3.0’ process ([Fig. 4](#F4)) could work akin to the ‘reverse vaccinology’ process that was used to develop the *Neisseria meningococcus* type B vaccine[233](#R233), but would be significantly quicker and would have a much higher throughput (‘reverse vaccinology 2.0’ was defined as rational vaccine design based on structural analysis of the interaction between the immune system and antigens[234](#R234)). Importantly, the final vaccine formulation can potentially be based on another vaccine platform (for example, recombinant protein vaccine) once the correct protective target antigen has been identified using mRNA technology.

##### Box 4 |. GMP manufacturing of mRNA vaccines.

Although Good Manufacturing Practice (GMP) production processes are usually complex, mRNA–lipid nanoparticle (LNP) manufacturing has major advantages compared to alternative vaccine production technologies. First, the components and process for manufacturing are well defined and there is no need for the use of eukaryotic cells, which decreases concerns about adventitious agents. Typically, DNA templates coding for the mRNA to be produced are synthesized and then cloned into an appropriate mRNA production plasmid[271](#R271)–[273](#R273). The plasmid is then amplified in an appropriate *Escherichia coli* strain, purified and used as a template for in vitro transcription of the antigen-encoding mRNA, driven by a recombinant RNA polymerase in a buffer containing the nucleotide-triphosphate molecules. Capping can be performed in the same reaction or in a downstream step[28](#R28),[29](#R29). mRNA needs to be purified to remove abortive unwanted RNA species[42](#R42),[44](#R44) as well as the DNA template (often via DNase digestion). Assays used for quality control at this step may include testing for DNA content, protein content, traces of double-stranded RNA, bioburden, endotoxin content and others. Once the mRNA is produced, it needs to be encapsulated into LNPs that are chemically well-defined mRNA vaccine components. This final product then undergoes further testing (for example, for particle size and encapsulation efficiency but also for bioburden and endotoxin content). Additional assays include confirmation of expression of the antigen in cell transfection studies as well as potency or immunogenicity tests. The topic is extensively discussed in reviews about the GMP manufacturing process of mRNA vaccines[271](#R271)–[274](#R274). A second significant advantage of the mRNA vaccine technology compared to the production of other vaccine modalities is the lack of a need for tedious process optimization for each vaccine candidate or changing target antigen as done with vaccines produced in cellular systems (inactivated vaccines, live-attenuated vaccines, recombinant protein vaccines and vectored vaccines). These optimizations may include significant changes that count as a new process (for example, when a new cell line needs to be used or when the downstream process needs to be redesigned). For mRNA-LNP vaccine production, the same process can be typically used for most target antigens, making it an extremely versatile, efficient and easy-to-produce platform.

##### Fig. 4 |. The simplicity and speed of mRNA vaccine production: reverse vaccinology 3.0.

![Fig. 4 |](https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/cee2/13101425/e49a8e14abb7/nihms-2152818-f0004.jpg)

[Open in a new tab](figure/F4/)

**a**, For most classical vaccine platforms, processes including upstream processing, downstream processing, and release criteria and testing need to be developed for each individual antigen separately, and there are often significant differences between processes. mRNA production is mostly sequence independent; the same production procedure can be used to produce any protein antigen-encoding mRNA. This makes Good Manufacturing Practice production very flexible and much more cost-effective. **b**, The ease and flexibility of producing mRNA vaccines coding for many different antigens enables ‘reverse vaccinology 3.0’. As an example, all open reading frames (ORFs) of a complex pathogen with unknown protective antigens could be produced as mRNA vaccines and tested in an animal challenge model, and protective antigens can be quickly identified that can then be developed as vaccine candidates for more detailed preclinical and potentially clinical studies.

### Vaccine equity and global access

An important challenge for mRNA vaccines is global vaccine equity. During the COVID-19 pandemic, access to mRNA vaccines in low-income and middle-income countries (LMICs) was suboptimal and delayed[235](#R235),[236](#R236). Low-income countries were often supplied with viral-vectored and inactivated virus-based SARS-CoV-2 vaccines (or no vaccines at all)[237](#R237); this was due to pricing, access, availability of ultracold storage distribution networks, vaccine nationalism and vaccine diplomacy[238](#R238). The requirement for mRNA vaccines to be frozen for long-term storage can be problematic for vaccine rollout even in high-income countries. Development of mRNA vaccines that are stable at 4 °C or even at room temperature is in progress and such thermostable mRNA vaccines may further increase the global impact of the mRNA vaccine technology[239](#R239)–[243](#R243).

For mRNA vaccine technology to be globally available, it is also important to establish development and manufacturing capacity regionally across the globe. It is promising that many traditional vaccine producers and start-up companies in LMICs have begun to develop their own mRNA vaccine formulations and facilities[244](#R244). Initiatives have begun towards strengthening this effort[245](#R245),[246](#R246) but some mRNA vaccine manufacturers are already scaling back on their efforts for technology transfer to LMICs[247](#R247). It also remains to be seen whether potential intellectual property issues on mRNA and LNP technologies can be resolved to facilitate access for LMIC vaccine producers. A global distribution of mRNA vaccine developers and producers would undoubtedly increase pandemic preparedness and would allow LMICs to rely on their own vaccines instead of being dependent on high-income nations and their vaccine diplomacy.

## Conclusions and perspectives

Although RNA-based vaccines, especially nucleoside-modified mRNA-LNP formulations, showed great promise for years, it was the COVID-19 pandemic that moved them into the spotlight and led to the first licensed mRNA vaccines ([Box 1](#BX1)). However, we are far from achieving the full potential of RNA-based vaccines and further optimization is needed to address current limitations. In this regard, opportunities lie ahead to develop novel RNA vaccine formulations with increased immunogenicity and decreased reactogenicity, greater stability, and with the ability to induce mucosal immune responses. In addition to viruses, there is a need for mRNA vaccines against human pathogenic bacteria, fungi and parasites as well as vaccines capable of simultaneously protecting from several different pathogens. The flexibility and ease of mRNA vaccine design and manufacturing will likely revolutionize antigen selection and vaccine development and may make it possible to design vaccines for pathogens for which they have previously been elusive. A deeper understanding of the mechanisms of action of mRNA vaccines will guide the design of next-generation mRNA vaccines.

The mRNA vaccine technology holds great promise for the future of vaccine development, immunization, and pandemic preparedness and is already contributing to a new golden age in vaccinology.
